    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer5579649640113878942"><b>The quintessential account of the Second World War as seen by Winston Churchill, its greatest leader</b><br/><br/>As Prime Minister of Great Britain from 1940 to 1945, Winston Churchill was not only the most powerful player in World War II, but also the free world's most eloquent voice of defiance in the face of Nazi tyranny. Churchill's epic accounts of those times, remarkable for thei</span>
  <span id="freeText5579649640113878942" style="display:none"><b>The quintessential account of the Second World War as seen by Winston Churchill, its greatest leader</b><br /><br />As Prime Minister of Great Britain from 1940 to 1945, Winston Churchill was not only the most powerful player in World War II, but also the free world's most eloquent voice of defiance in the face of Nazi tyranny. Churchill's epic accounts of those times, remarkable for their grand sweep and incisive firsthand observations, are distilled here in a single essential volume. <i>Memoirs of the Second World War</i> is a vital and illuminating work that retains the drama, eyewitness details, and magisterial prose of his classic six-volume history and offers an invaluable view of pivotal events of the twentieth century.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13051456318090944656">The Second World War = Memoirs of the Second World War, Winston S. Churchill<br/> <br/>The Second World War is a history of the period from the end of the First World War to July 1945, written by Winston Churchill. Churchill labelled the "moral of the work" as follows: "In War: Resolution, In Defeat: Defiance, In Victory: Magnanimity, In Peace: Goodwill".<br/><br/>First edition (hardcover) in six volumes<br/>The Gathering Storm (1948)<br/>Their Finest Hour (1949)<br/>The Grand Alliance (1950)<br/>The Hinge of Fate (1950)<br/>Closing the Ri</span>
  <span id="freeText13051456318090944656" style="display:none">The Second World War = Memoirs of the Second World War, Winston S. Churchill<br /> <br />The Second World War is a history of the period from the end of the First World War to July 1945, written by Winston Churchill. Churchill labelled the "moral of the work" as follows: "In War: Resolution, In Defeat: Defiance, In Victory: Magnanimity, In Peace: Goodwill".<br /><br />First edition (hardcover) in six volumes<br />The Gathering Storm (1948)<br />Their Finest Hour (1949)<br />The Grand Alliance (1950)<br />The Hinge of Fate (1950)<br />Closing the Ring (1951)<br />Triumph and Tragedy (1953)<br /><br />Full Onslaught of Japan<br />Victory in Africa<br />The Invasion of Italy<br />Assault from the Air<br />The Tide of Victory<br />Triumph and Tragedy<br /><br />Condensed edition in four volumes<br />Milestones to Disaster<br />Alone<br />The Grand Alliance<br />Triumph and Tragedy<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش نسخه فارسی: سال 1973 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: خاطرات پرچیل؛ نویسنده: وینستون چرچیل؛ مترجم: ذبیح الله منصوری؛ تهران، صفیعلیشاه، 1334؛ جلد اول: طوف‍ان‌ ن‍زدی‍ک‌ م‍ی‍ش‍ود؛ جلد دوم خ‍اطرات‌ چ‍رچ‍ی‍ل‌؛ جلد سوم و چهارم: ات‍ح‍اد ب‍زرگ‌؛ جلد پنجم: ام‍ری‍ک‍ا وارد ج‍ن‍گ‌ م‍ی‍ش‍ود "ح‍م‍ل‍ه‌ ب‍ای‍ران‌"؛ جلد ششم: ت‍غ‍ی‍ی‍ر س‍رن‍وش‍ت‌، ح‍م‍ل‍ه‌ ژاپ‍ن‌ از هجدهم ژان‍وی‍ه‌ ت‍ا سوم ماه ژوی‍ه سال‌ 1942میلادی؛ جلد هفتم: نجات افریقا؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، نگارستان کتاب، در دو جلد، 1389؛ شابک دوره 9786005541847؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، نگارستان کتاب، در سه جلد، 1397؛ شابک دوره 9786001901126؛ موضوع: یادمانهای نویسندگان بریتانیایی - سده 20 م<br /><br />عنوان: خاطرات جنگ جهانی دوم؛ نویسنده: وینستون چرچیل؛ مترجم: تورج فرازمند؛ تهران، شش جلد در ده مجلد با عنوانهای: مجلد یک: حلد یک، طوف‍ان‌ ن‍زدی‍ک‌ م‍ی‌ش‍ود؛ مجلد دوم: جلد دو، ل‍ح‍ظات‌ ح‍س‍اس‌؛ مجلد سوم: جلد سه. ق‌ یک. ه‍ج‍وم‌ ب‍ه‌ روس‍ی‍ه‌؛ مجلد چهارم: جلد سه، ق‌ دوم: آم‍ری‍ک‍ا در ج‍ن‍گ‌؛ مجلد پنجم: جلد چهار، ق‌ یک: ج‍ن‍گ‌ ژاپ‍ن‌؛ مجلد ششم: جلد چهار، ق‌ دوم: ن‍ج‍ات‌ آف‍ری‍ق‍ا؛ مجلد هفتم: جلد پنج، ق‌ یک: ای‍ت‍ال‍ی‍ا ت‍س‍ل‍ی‍م‌ م‍ی‌ش‍ود؛ مجلد هشتم: جلد پنج، قسمت دوم: از ت‍ه‍ران‌ ت‍ا روم‌؛ مجلد نهم: ؛ مجلد دهم: ؛ چاپ نخست: تهران، نیل، از سال 1339 تا سال 1347؛ چاپ دیگر 1361؛ موضوع یادمانهای جنگ جهانی دوم از نویسندگان بریتانیایی - سده 20م<br /><br />تاریخ جنگ جهانی دوم مجموعه کتابی شش جلدی، دربردانده ی شرح رخدادهای جنگ جهانی دوم، از دیدگاه «وینستون چرچیل» است، که رخدادهای آن از سال پایان جنگ اول جهانی، تا ماه ژوئیه سال 1945میلادی را، در بر می‌گیرد؛ از رهبران کشورهای عمده ی درگیر در جنگ دوم (هیتلر، موسولینی، استالین و روزولت)، تنها «چرچیل» بودند که کتابی تاریخی منتشر کردند؛ برخلاف خبرنگاران، که تنها از مسایل علنی آگاه می‌شدند، «چرچیل»، در اموری همانند گفتگوهای محرمانه، و طراحی‌های عملیات نظامی شرکت داشته‌ اند، و به جزئیات رویدادها آگاهی داشته‌ اند؛ افزون بر این، ایشان به گزارشهای سازمانهای اطلاعاتی «بریتانیا»، و بویژه نتایج عملیات «اولترا»، که در مکالمات سری ارتش «آلمان»، رخنه کرده بود، دسترسی داشته اند، و می‌توانستند تصویری روشنتر، از رخدادهای جنگی، و انگیزه‌ های رهبران کشورها به دست بدهند؛ «چرچیل» از آغاز جنگ، قصد داشته تا کتابی تاریخی، درباره ی آن بنویسند، و از همینروی به صورت هفتگی، گزارشهایی از خلاصه ی رخدادها تهیه می‌کرده اند، و در منزل خویش آن گزارشها را نگهداری مینمودند؛ برخی باور دارند، نگارش کتاب تنها به دست «چرچیل» انجام نشده، و به راستی کتاب نتیجه ی کار گروهی از پژوهشگران است، که زیر نظر «چرچیل»، و با سود بردن از نوشته‌ ها و یادداشتهای ایشان، به این کتاب بزرگوار هستی بخشیده‌ اند؛ در برابر این باور، دیگرانی نیز باور دارند، که موشکافی و حساسیت «چرچیل»، اجازه ی چنین کاری را نمیداده، و بیشتر همکاران ایشان، تنها در تهیه ی اسناد و مدارک مورد استفاده، به ایشان یاری رسانده‌ اند؛<br /><br />نخستین چاپ این مجموعه کتاب، به صورت مجموعه ای شش جلدی بوده‌ است؛ جلد نخست با عنوان: («طوفان نزدیک می‌شود، سال انتشار 1948میلادی»، دربردارنده ی مطالبیست که «چرچیل» در دهه ی سی، از سده بیستم میلادی بنوشته بودند، و قصد داشتند، در کتابی درباره ی رخدادهای پس از سال 1919میلادی چاپ کنند؛ این کتاب در برگیرنده ی دیدگاههای انتقادی ایشان، درباره ی سیاست مماشات دولتهای «بالدوین»، و «چمبرلین» می‌باشد)؛<br /><br />عنوان جلد دوم: («عالی‌ترین ساعت آنها، سال انتشار 1949میلادی»؛ نقل از سخنرانی مشهور روز هجدهم ماه ژوئن سال 1940میلادی چرچیل: «باشد که چنان به وظایف و مسولیتهای خود عمل کنیم که اگر امپراطوری بریتانیا و کشورهای مشترک‌ المنافع هزار سال هم بر پا بمانند، همگان بگویند که این عالی‌ترین ساعت آن‌ها بوده‌ است.» پایان نقل؛ این جلد شامل شرح پیشرویهای آلمان در غرب، و دوران دشوار پس از سقوط فرانسه می‌باشد، که با پایداری بریتانیا و انصراف آلمان از عملیات «شیر دریایی» پایان می‌باید). <br /><br />جلد سوم («اتحاد بزرگ، سال انتشار 1950میلادی»، شامل شرح گسترش جنگ، از اروپا به دیگر نقاط جهان، از جمله حمله ی آلمان به شوروی، و ورود آمریکا به جنگ، در سال 1941میلادی می‌باشد.)؛ <br /><br />در جلد چهارم: «چرخش سرنوشت، سال انتشار 1950میلادی»؛<br /><br />جلد پنجم: «بسته شدن حلقه، سال انتشار 1951 میلادی»؛<br /><br /> و جلد ششم: «پیروزی و تراژدی، سال انتشار 1953»، به رخدادهای سالهای افول قدرت ارتش هیتلر، پس از شکست استالینگراد، شرکت وسیع آمریکا در عملیاتهای نظامی، و تصمیم‌گیری درباره سرنوشت جنگ، تصمیم گیریهای کنفرانسهای: «تهران»، «یالتا» و «پتسدام»، و روابط با «شوروی»، و سرنوشت «آلمان»، «لهستان»، «یونان»، و دیگر کشورهای درگیر در جنگ، پس از جنگ پرداخته می‌شود؛<br /><br />علاوه بر دوره شش جلدی، این کتاب در دوره‌ های دوازده جلدی، و چهار جلدی نیز منتشر شده‌ است؛ این کتاب را نخستین بار روانشاد «ذبیح الله منصوری» ترجمه کردند؛ (البته ایشان تنها هفت جلد از دوره دوازده جلدی را ترجمه کردند)؛ کتاب در دوازده جلد، با ترجمه جناب آقای «تورج فرازمند» به سال 1347هجری خورشیدی، از سوی انتشارات نیل به فارسی منتشر شده‌ است<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 31/03/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="13051456318090944656" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3076933792" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3076933792" />
    <a title="Markus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48327873-markus"><img alt="Markus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449341848p2/48327873.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3076933792?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Markus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Markus" href="/user/show/48327873-markus">Markus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780395522622" href="/book/show/692208.Memoirs_of_the_Second_World_War">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48327873-markus?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48327873-markus?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3076933792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer190843721168267272">Churchill’s Memoirs of World War II<br/>By Winston Churchill (1874-1965)<br/><br/><br/>The present edition is an abridged version of the original, which amounts to six volumes.<br/>It is the report from Winston Churchill, who shaped and observed history as an actor and eyewitness in the front seat.<br/><br/>The world has narrowly escaped a destiny which could have been shaped by Nazi Germany if they had won.<br/><br/>A few very fortunate circumstances made the difference. <br/><br/>These circumstances are outlined by the author of these memoirs, <br/>som</span>
  <span id="freeText190843721168267272" style="display:none">Churchill’s Memoirs of World War II<br />By Winston Churchill (1874-1965)<br /><br /><br />The present edition is an abridged version of the original, which amounts to six volumes.<br />It is the report from Winston Churchill, who shaped and observed history as an actor and eyewitness in the front seat.<br /><br />The world has narrowly escaped a destiny which could have been shaped by Nazi Germany if they had won.<br /><br />A few very fortunate circumstances made the difference. <br /><br />These circumstances are outlined by the author of these memoirs, <br />some of which, as the most striking to me, I would like to quote hereafter.<br /><br />But first, it is necessary to remember the extraordinary situation between the two wars from 1918 to 1939.<br /><br />There is the incredible treachery of the Germans after the Treaty of Versailles of 1918.<br /><br />Then there is the unbelievable naivety and ignorance of the neighbouring countries who did not see and would not believe that the rebuilding of the German war machine at high speed was not a bluff, but to go to war.<br /><br />In March 1933 Hitler opened the first Reichstag of the Third Reich. <br />On March 24 the absolute majority of the Reichstag confirmed by 441 votes to 94 full emergency powers to Chancellor Hitler for four years. <br /><br />Under Hitler’s orders, the German war industry was hurled into full speed.<br />Irrespective of any limitations set out in the Treaty of Versailles at the end of WWI.<br /><br />When the British and French Government finally started to realise the danger, a Disarmament Conference was called up. <br /><br />Hitler did not even trouble to study the offers presented to him. <br />He just withdrew from the Conference and from the League of Nations, with a gesture of disdain.<br />Americans remained aloof and merely shrugged their shoulders. They were to change their attitude later.<br /><br />In 1931 the British government under PM Baldwin without consulting the French ally or the League of Nations made a secret agreement with Hitler allowing the Germans to build <br />U-boats explicitly denied in the Peace treaty!<br />This resulted in that, over the coming four years of war, more than fourteen and a half million tonnes of allied shipping were sunk by German U-boats alone!<br /><br />It was only when Hitler unleashed his hellhounds against his nearest and weakest neighbours, 1938 Austria and 1939 Poland; Norway was invaded and in German control.<br />In 1940, France was invaded on a fast pace and soon ceased to fight. Marechal Petain signed the Armistice with the Germans. <br /><br />The British realised that they were going to be the next target; and that they were in for trouble because ill-prepared.<br /><br />Then London had its first bombing raids by German aircraft.<br /><br />Now the first of the fortunate events took place. <br />On the 10th Mai 1940, Winston Churchill was named Minister of War and Admiralty. <br />He was a man with a stubborn and robust character and the necessary experience from WWI. The right man at the right place at the right time.<br /><br />But Britain could not challenge Germany on its own. It needed help from America.<br /><br />President Roosevelt had proposed to PM Chamberlain to set up a meeting to discuss the European situation. At first, Chamberlain had arrogantly with a wave of one hand, declined. <br />But soon the situation became dramatic. And the British attitude changed.<br /><br />American aid shipments started to flow; arms and war material.<br />Throughout the war, these shipments were most dramatically sabotaged by German submarines, sinking countless ships.<br /><br />In December 1941 the other, for Europe, “fortunate event” happened: Pearl Harbour!<br />And three days later, Germany declared war on America.<br /><br />The Japanese bombing of the US harbour of Pearl Harbour and the German declaration of war gave President Roosevelt the decisive argument to obtain from Congress the green light to officially declare war on Japan and join WWII against Hitler.<br /><br />Europe did not have the means of winning the war against Germany without the intervention of the American Navy, Airforce and Army. <br />This was demonstrated by the historic D-Day landing on the beaches of Normandy in Northern France in June 1944.<br /><br />The unimaginable size of American War Industry came into full operation. <br />Roosevelt called to produce 45,000 combat aircraft, 45,000 tanks, 20,000 anti-aircraft guns, 14,900 anti-tank guns and 500,000 machine guns. <br />These figures were surpassed in 1943.<br />1942 shipping tonnage built 5,339,000 tons<br />1943 shipping tonnage built 12,384,000 tons<br /><br />Then Hitler made the same mistake Napoleon made in 1812. <br /><br />In June 1941 he declared war on Russia, underestimating the sheer endless size of Russian territory, the unbearable cold and snow of winter, and the formidable size and fighting will-power of the Red Army. Russian arms and munitions were outdated, but this was soon to change. <br /><br />Throughout 1942 and 1943 British navy dispatched 40 convoys over the Arctic waters with much-needed supplies to Russia, to help against the German aggression.<br />Amounting to a total worth of 428,000,000 pounds of material, including 5000 tanks and over 7000 aircraft.<br />The Royal Navy paid a heavy price: Two cruisers and seventeen other warships were sunk by German U-boats. 1840 officers and men died.<br /><br />It would make this review too long if I went into more military and political details, like the battles in North Africa, and Italy, the political disasters of the Balkan states and about the war in Asia against Japan and the Atomic bomb there.<br /><br />In the last chapter of this book, ‘Triumph and Tragedy.’ Churchill writes about his disappointment with the outcome after the demise of Germany. <br /><br />The much too hasty withdrawal of the American Army from halfway across Germany, leaving enormous territories to be recovered by Russia and setting the conditions for the Iron Curtain. <br />Contrary to all agreements signed with Moscow, the Communist occupants of the Eastern countries refused any Western observers to enter and see the conditions of Elections and Governments established. <br /><br />The other fortunate event worth mentioning is that in the end, the victory for the Allies came none too soon.<br />If the war had, for any reason dragged on much longer, the outcome might still have been in favour of the Germans.<br /><br />Even though against the end, they were running out of raw materials, like gasoline, steel and rubber, their progress in developing new weapons was continuous and frightening.<br /><br />Germany’s advanced weapons in 1944 were among others, the V2 rocket or flying bombs – New high-speed U-boats – Jet propulsion aeroplanes. <br />How close the German research had come close to the A-bomb is a controversy still under discussion.<br /><br />This abridged version does not mention the Concentration camps of Auschwitz or others.<br />Nor does it report on the Nürnberg War Tribunal where the German Generals were condemned to death by hanging. Hitler had ended his life by suicide.<br /><br />At the end of the book, Churchill provides his thoughts for the future of Europe.<br /><br />“…I am now going to say something that will astonish you. The first step in the re-creation of the European family must be a partnership between France and Germany. In this way only can France recover the moral leadership of Europe. There can be no revival of Europe without a spiritually great France and a spiritually great Germany. The structure of the United States of Europe, if well and truly built it, will be such as to make the material strength of a single state less important. (…) if we are to form the United States of Europe or whatever name or form it may take, we must begin now.” (1946).<br /><br />This abridged edition may give enough details of this historical period for the average reader.<br />But for a history nerd like me, I look forward to reading the full set of six, which I was fortunate enough in finding a good 1948 edition. <br /><br />In 1953 Churchill was honoured with the Nobel Prize of Literature for this work.<br />Enjoy your reading!</span>
  <a data-text-id="190843721168267272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/119327139?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/4215097-john">John</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4215097-john?shelf=history-biography">history-biography</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119327139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14359517860463983339">I really admire Churchill as a war leader. He was exactly the right man in the right job for the time. But if you want accurate history, go elsewhere. Since millions of documents have been declassified, especially the existence of ULTRA, any WWII history released before 1980 is by definition, woefully incomplete. Like Eisenhower's war memoir, Churchill's is intended to secure his place in history, not at all to tell an accurate story. (Additionally, the main reason Churchill wrote these books is</span>
  <span id="freeText14359517860463983339" style="display:none">I really admire Churchill as a war leader. He was exactly the right man in the right job for the time. But if you want accurate history, go elsewhere. Since millions of documents have been declassified, especially the existence of ULTRA, any WWII history released before 1980 is by definition, woefully incomplete. Like Eisenhower's war memoir, Churchill's is intended to secure his place in history, not at all to tell an accurate story. (Additionally, the main reason Churchill wrote these books is for the money. He was broke.) <br /><br />But this book has value none-the-less for Churchill's powerful point of view. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14359517860463983339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1545370566?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clark" href="/user/show/1731590-clark">Clark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/20565767-memoirs-of-the-second-world-war">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1731590-clark?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1731590-clark?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1731590-clark?shelf=25-all-time-favorites">25-all-time-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1545370566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15184963054614590168">It took me several weeks to read this book of over 1000 pages, but it was well worth it. Perhaps the best book I have ever read, both for enlightenment and educational value. For me, Winston Churchill is the greatest man to have lived during my lifetime.<br/><br/>Some things I learned reading this book:<br/><br/>•	Amazed to learn about the effect of the U-boats in WWII. Of the total fleet of 250, close to 100 were in U.S. waters at one time during 1942 & 1943. In 68 months of fighting 781 German U-boats were lost, </span>
  <span id="freeText15184963054614590168" style="display:none">It took me several weeks to read this book of over 1000 pages, but it was well worth it. Perhaps the best book I have ever read, both for enlightenment and educational value. For me, Winston Churchill is the greatest man to have lived during my lifetime.<br /><br />Some things I learned reading this book:<br /><br />•	Amazed to learn about the effect of the U-boats in WWII. Of the total fleet of 250, close to 100 were in U.S. waters at one time during 1942 &amp; 1943. In 68 months of fighting 781 German U-boats were lost, but over 14 million tons of Allied shipping were sunk by U-boats<br /><br />•	Learned that Churchill was constantly travelling during the war and preferred face-to-face meetings to communicate. As Prime Minister from 1940 to 1945, he made at least twenty-five trips outside Britain, some ranging over several continents, including several to the U.S. to meet with President Roosevelt. <br /><br />•	He also liked to be close to the battlefield. He visited Normandy 4 days after D-Day and had lunch in a tent 3 miles from the actual enemy lines. He was with Field Marshall Montgomery when the British Army crossed the Rhine on March 23, 1945, and watched as four divisions crossed the river. <br /><br />•	Churchill supported 100% the dropping of the atomic bombs on Hiroshima and Nagasaki, primarily because the Japanese power was in the hands of the military, which were determined to commit the nation to mass suicide rather than accept defeat. Both he and Truman felt over one million U.S. soldiers and one-half million British would have been killed if it were necessary to invade Japan to gain their surrender.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15184963054614590168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_364469945" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/364469945" />
    <a title="Janet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/523081-janet"><img alt="Janet" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/364469945?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janet" href="/user/show/523081-janet">Janet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer364469945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9870027758881496739">A fascinating personal narrative, as opposed to a history, of WWII. WSC believed in the British Empire and a special relationship among all "English-speaking people." The first half of the abridged version of his six-volume memoirs is stronger; as the war drags on he focuses on the many conferences of the "Big Three" FDR, WSC and Stalin. If everyone had just heeded all his warnings and had implemented his strategies all would be well, or at least better. As Germany is collapsing toward surrender</span>
  <span id="freeText9870027758881496739" style="display:none">A fascinating personal narrative, as opposed to a history, of WWII. WSC believed in the British Empire and a special relationship among all "English-speaking people." The first half of the abridged version of his six-volume memoirs is stronger; as the war drags on he focuses on the many conferences of the "Big Three" FDR, WSC and Stalin. If everyone had just heeded all his warnings and had implemented his strategies all would be well, or at least better. As Germany is collapsing toward surrender, WSC never mentions the Nazi death camps and seems more concerned about the Polish borders. Still it's amazing he was voted out of office at the end of July 1945 and didn't have the opportunity to stand up to the Russians in the immediate post-war period.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9870027758881496739" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_138675188" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/138675188" />
    <a title="Don Stanton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2836068-don-stanton"><img alt="Don Stanton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1266436047p2/2836068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/138675188?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Don Stanton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Don Stanton" href="/user/show/2836068-don-stanton">Don Stanton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer138675188" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7990773335406630332">Clearly an essential book to read, especially if there is an interest on how Great Britain survived the years of war prior to the American full involvement. The details of desperate decisions, know that his decision would cost more British lives, but for the greater good. I could see the transformation from a gentle soul to what he was called later, 'the Lion,'or the "Great Lion." How must it have felt that the future of his nation hung on his every word.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6208093" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6208093" />
    <a title="Scottie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/374348-scottie"><img alt="Scottie" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6208093?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scottie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scottie" href="/user/show/374348-scottie">Scottie</a>
      </span>

          added it



          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone wanting to learn about WWII</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6208093" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18276303205658187184">Although I was vaguely aware that the Second World War encompassed more than Germany and Russia, I had no idea of the vast expanses in the war's theatre. The campaign across North Africa was particularly compelling for me insofar as I had an uncle who fought in that campaign. Churchill's abridged memoirs run well past 1000+ pages, often laborious readings, but straight from the horse's mouth.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2596263539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2596263539" />
    <a title="Mike Blackwell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21088891-mike-blackwell"><img alt="Mike Blackwell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456121764p2/21088891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2596263539?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Blackwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Blackwell" href="/user/show/21088891-mike-blackwell">Mike Blackwell</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517270325" href="/book/show/3651486-memoirs-of-the-second-world-war">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21088891-mike-blackwell?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21088891-mike-blackwell?shelf=world-wars">world-wars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21088891-mike-blackwell?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21088891-mike-blackwell?shelf=britain">britain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2596263539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1684492429239374419">This is a book of military history, for the most part. Churchill is a politician, obviously, but his political views are only expressed when they overlap with the military situation, and they are steeped in archaic, imperialist thinking. More on that later. <br/><br/>This book is about Britain. It is a book about how Britain went to war in Europe, was joined by the Americans and the Russians, and fought back the Nazis. It covers that wonderfully. You get to hear a first-hand account of all the major confe</span>
  <span id="freeText1684492429239374419" style="display:none">This is a book of military history, for the most part. Churchill is a politician, obviously, but his political views are only expressed when they overlap with the military situation, and they are steeped in archaic, imperialist thinking. More on that later. <br /><br />This book is about Britain. It is a book about how Britain went to war in Europe, was joined by the Americans and the Russians, and fought back the Nazis. It covers that wonderfully. You get to hear a first-hand account of all the major conferences, all the arguments about large strategic decisions, and by the end, have a much deeper understanding of the logistics of maintaining a war over such a large area for such a long time. That, I think, is the main value of this book. It is truly mind-boggling the amount of manpower and technology that was put into waging this war. War is not about morality; it is not about honour; it is, at least in the 20th century adaptation, a question of supply lines, factory production, and political agreements. <br /><br />Another aspect of the war that Churchill shines a lot of light on is the Battle of the Atlantic, with the US and British navy fighting to maintain their trans-Atlantic shipping from the U-boat menace. This is not an especially glamorous or engaging aspect of the fighting, but its importance is hard to overstate. <br /><br />I found the sections dealing with Stalin the most interesting. He really played his cards for all he could. The frustration is palpable as Stalin and Molotov continually twist the US and UK with their arguments about Russia's extreme casualty numbers, and the question of Russian "security" in post-war Europe. At the end of the war, when Eastern Europe has traded totalitarian Nazi rule for totalitarian Soviet rule, it's clear just how demoralizing this is for Churchill. It's so hard to argue that things could have been done differently. It's a lot to think about. <br /><br />When Churchill is talking about Western Europe, his views are insightful and interesting. Anywhere outside of that, his imperialism shines through clearly. The epilogue includes opinions about India, the Middle East, and Indochina that were unfortunately all too common at the time: namely, that these people can't be trusted to govern themselves, and their cries for independence and self-governance should not have been listened to. This is in direct contrast to his struggles throughout the later part of the war to maintain the liberty of Greece and Poland (with varying results). <br /><br />Also, Churchill just didn't give a shit at all about China, and it shows. <br /><br />Even the question of what the Nazis did is barely covered. If you didn't know better, you'd think that the only problem with Nazi Germany was that they wanted to take over Poland. Even at the end of the war, Churchill constantly references Poland as "the whole reason we had begun the war" when trying to reclaim its sovereignty from foreign influence. I mean, this is true to an extent, but...<br /><br />Definitely an interesting book, but I would encourage anyone interested to consider it as supplementary to other overviews of the war, considering its inherent biases and the fact that, having been written during the decade following the war, a lot of its information has been superseded by further research.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1684492429239374419" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3193146422" />
    <a title="DALE Sabo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/105507340-dale-sabo"><img alt="DALE Sabo" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3193146422?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DALE Sabo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DALE Sabo" href="/user/show/105507340-dale-sabo">DALE Sabo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780395522622" href="/book/show/692208.Memoirs_of_the_Second_World_War">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3193146422" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3139907654357989945">Just over a thousand pages long, this is Churchill’s own abridgment of his six volumes of The Second World War. It begins in 1919 and the epilogue ends in 1957. The author was at the center of much of this terrible epic history. Like a number of Englishmen he has a great gift for written English. <br/><br/>He relates how the West threw away several chances to easily stop Hitler in the 1930’s, and only finally openly opposed the Nazis after they’d swallowed 1/4 of Europe one nation at a time. It is a remin</span>
  <span id="freeText3139907654357989945" style="display:none">Just over a thousand pages long, this is Churchill’s own abridgment of his six volumes of The Second World War. It begins in 1919 and the epilogue ends in 1957. The author was at the center of much of this terrible epic history. Like a number of Englishmen he has a great gift for written English. <br /><br />He relates how the West threw away several chances to easily stop Hitler in the 1930’s, and only finally openly opposed the Nazis after they’d swallowed 1/4 of Europe one nation at a time. It is a reminder of great bravery in the face of great evil, especially the period from the fall of France in June 1940 to the German invasion of Russia in June of 1941 when Great Britain stood alone and the Nazis seemed triumphant everywhere. <br /><br />This is an excellent story of the politics, diplomacy and military events. He’s honest in citing his own mistakes and those of others, given his perspective. He was an unapologetic conservative Englishman who favored empire as a young man and commonwealth as an old man. He recognized we are all prisoners of our time to some degree. The book is surprisingly valuable in understanding the years before and after the war. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3139907654357989945" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12516184967955991512">I've been reading this in chunks of 200 pages since the spring. Churchill's telling of the war time meetings with FDR and Stalin and the situation Britain faced from the start of the war through the Battle of Britain are the highlights of the book.   <span id="freeText12516184967955991512" style="display:none">I've been reading this in chunks of 200 pages since the spring. Churchill's telling of the war time meetings with FDR and Stalin and the situation Britain faced from the start of the war through the Battle of Britain are the highlights of the book. I appreciated new insights into Britain's role in the war in the Mediterranean and what happened in Greece and the Balkan's in the vacuum after the German's were defeated. Particularly interesting was Churchill's vision of how the victors would allow the people of the liberated countries to chart their own future following agreements he thought he had with the Soviets after meetings in Tehran and how Britain took an active role in Greece to stop a communist insurgency. This was contrasted with how the Soviets used their military position and power to support puppet governments in the Balkans and then Poland. Actions that weren't reversed until 40 to 50 years later. Good history!</span>

<div id="review_3573609326" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer11727067008062750182">I read this many years ago and someday I may have ample time to read the unabridged volumes. This is an excellent account of Churchill's philosophy and decision making as he led his nation and at times the world through these tumultuous years. His power with the English language and the clarity of his resolve are evident.</span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3573609326">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2984582159" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14006174857191296218">Very interesting primary source about WW2 and politics. Of course you can't take Mr. Churchill's account as the complete and absolute truth, but if you're interested in gaining a deep understanding of those world events, you need to go through this. Certainly the fact he knows how to spin a tale and his mastery of english language make it an easy read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3122155168330829090" style="display:none">The following is a note I wrote on FB about it.<br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------<br />Finished reading today "Memoirs of the second World War" by Winston S. Churchill.<br /><br />Magnificent 1000 pages.<br /><br />Most of the times you will be awed by the greatness of the man, you will see how mankind fell to its its own follies. You will see how easy is it to give in, but above all there are some principles which should always adhere to. <br /><br />When your actions and decisions would affect freedom of all the people of the world and alter the course human civilisation, how would you conduct yourself. There are times when things look dangerously bleak and the world (literally) strikes you off. What do you do, left alone to face your mortal enemy outnumbering you in quality and quantity?<br /><br />There are principles which we must all adhere to even in the face of gravest dangers. And if you waver from the basic principles of war and humanity it will ultimately spell you doom.<br /><br />In the meantime we see the ridiculous efficiency with which the first Great Britain and henceforth allies conducted their war machine. How a nation so divided in times of peace comes together in the hour of peril, you see that sacrifice, bravery, solidarity, comradeship, loyalty and all these principles really do matter.<br /><br />Lots of excellent managerial lessons for aspiring managers.<br /><br />How much is dependent on luck, in a span of minutes the Battle of Britain was decided, without firing a single shot the domination in the pacific.<br /><br />Why couldn't germans cross the English Channel but the British could do so, was it just brute force, no it was fore-sight and proper planning. How most of our lives are wasted worrying about things that never happen, and so much is dependent on luck that we cannot hope to do anything else but do our best.<br /><br />You will be filled by anguish as you see the rush for the spoils again, you will shake your head a hundred times, and you would realise that world could have been a far different place. And it would have been far worse if some people did not stand up for what they believed in.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3122155168330829090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8332277682538701404">A very good book. I have always had more interest in the british effort in the second world war than from the US and the USSR, specially considering how underrated the britsh war effort is. This book dwelves into all the fronts, obviously giving emphasis on how britain was affected or what its actions was.<br/><br/>One of the most interesting parts was a graphic that showed how the UK had actually more divisions on the frontlines (that is, the india army does not counts, nor those divisions in england and</span>
  <span id="freeText8332277682538701404" style="display:none">A very good book. I have always had more interest in the british effort in the second world war than from the US and the USSR, specially considering how underrated the britsh war effort is. This book dwelves into all the fronts, obviously giving emphasis on how britain was affected or what its actions was.<br /><br />One of the most interesting parts was a graphic that showed how the UK had actually more divisions on the frontlines (that is, the india army does not counts, nor those divisions in england and etc, but Slim's 14th army in Burma does) than the US up until June 1944. <br /><br />That is after more than 500k axis forces had already surrendered or died in Africa(where in east africa it was all british and north africa only the last months saw the actions of 1 US and 1 french corps on contrast with 4 british corps, 3 which inflicted the great german defeat in the front which was El Alamein), the german surface navy completely destroyed, the surviving italian navy on allied hands after 1943, half of italy liberated, and the tide completely turned in the eastern front by the Soviet Union.<br /><br /><br />This book does justice to the british war effort. Britain stood alone in 1940 with all of continental Europe under german or its allies occupation, and yet throughout this year and a half, they won the battle of britain, operation compass, held malta, continued to destroy the german surface navy such as the sinking of the bismarck in 1941, won the battle of keren and destroyed the italian presence on east africa, won the syria and lebanon campaign and nullified the possibility of any german advance into the middle east as well as won the Anglo Iraqi war, which both secured the most strategically important oil fields in the world, and won decisive naval victories in the battles of Taranto and Cape Matapan in the mediterranean.<br /><br />In fact, its only defeat was Greece and Crete. And giving the odds, how could it be different.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2021936206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16995044544119004498">I'm not really sure how to review this. It's exactly what it says it is - 1,000+ pages of Churchill talking about WWII. It's well-written, and interesting if you like reading about history or war or geopolitics. Definitely interesting to hear about the behind-the-scenes conversations and negotiations that took place between the various world leaders. From what I have heard, it's a bit self-serving in that it paints Churchill in the best light possible. I don't doubt that, but it's still incredib</span>
  <span id="freeText16995044544119004498" style="display:none">I'm not really sure how to review this. It's exactly what it says it is - 1,000+ pages of Churchill talking about WWII. It's well-written, and interesting if you like reading about history or war or geopolitics. Definitely interesting to hear about the behind-the-scenes conversations and negotiations that took place between the various world leaders. From what I have heard, it's a bit self-serving in that it paints Churchill in the best light possible. I don't doubt that, but it's still incredibly informative. As with anything, you're better off reading multiple things to get a realistic view rather than relying on a single source. Most people with a casual interest in WWII will probably find this to be way too long and involved, particularly about some of the less well-known battles and historical figures. It does give you an appreciation for the weight of the decisions that had to be made and the far-reaching consequences that had to be considered. It made me less willing to pass judgment on the leaders involved.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16995044544119004498" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText353905203094265768" style="display:none">This is an exceptional read for anyone interested in the history of WWII. It is a condensed version, as opposed to the original 6 volume set. Yet, it portrays well the political and economic climate that led up to WWII, and the on goings from Churchill's perspective. <br /><br />I read this book and do agree there were some things omitted, especially regarding ULTRA. Read this book and then the "Bodyguard of Lies" by Anthony Cave Brown, which details what Churchill could not due to the secrets act. Kept as secret documents until well after Churchill's death, the Bodyguard of Lies fills in the gaps and weaves a story of espionage, deception, and treachery detailing just how much the Allies actually knew about Axis operations. <br /><br /> <br /><br /> <br /><br /></span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8750759885387444957">I have always wanted to read the six book series and actually picked this up by accident, not realizing it was an abridgement. I am glad I did! I enjoy the political and social aspects of the book, but not the detailed military narratives. <br/><br/>Churchill certainly has an agenda in recounting certain aspects of the allied strategy, but I enjoyed in nonetheless. </span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TLpCDnRbuyMlbIAJPmF2qpYoZB8RZdvr6Y1DfRBVF+Dn7RE4FIJLyKNtg8SBG1jv9S/WmV+3h7Ccg8fSi5d0jg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('F7jZ2ACNhIeEWHCxX64ixC6fjDvcqkplGOsH3mAGU8a874ruYFR0bAJZc3zg1AyBTZg+vZJ4Fj5t5YNx+8QwqA==')})
  }

O livro foi publicado no Porto em 1888. A acção de Os Maias passa-se em Lisboa, na segunda metade do século XIX, e apresent<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7816966755593019147\" style=\"display:none\">Biblioteca Ulisseia de Autores Portugueses #15\n\nOs Maias é uma das obras mais conhecidas do escritor português Eça de Queiroz. O livro foi publicado no Porto em 1888. A acção de Os Maias passa-se em Lisboa, na segunda metade do século XIX, e apresenta-nos a história de três gerações da família Maia. A acção inicia-se no Outono de 1875, quando Afonso da Maia, nobre e pobre proprietário, se instala no Ramalhete com o neto recém formado em Medicina. Neste momento faz-se uma longa descrição da casa - &quot;O Ramalhete,&quot; cujo nome tem origem num painel de azulejos com um ramo de girassóis, e não em algo fresco ou campestre, tal como o nome nos remete a pensar. Afonso da Maia era o personagem mais simpático do romance e aquele que o autor mais valorizou, pois não se lhe conhecem defeitos. É um homem de carácter, culto e requintado nos gostos. Em jovem aderiu aos ideais do Liberalismo e foi obrigado, por seu pai, a sair de casa e a instalar-se em Inglaterra. Após o pai falecer regressa a Lisboa para casar com Maria Eduarda Runa, mas pouco tempo depois escolhe o exílio por razões de ordem política. Há em Os Maias um retrato da Lisboa da epóca. Carlos, que mora na Rua das Janelas Verdes, caminha com frequência até ao Rossio (embora, por vezes, vá a cavalo ou de carruagem). Algumas das lojas citadas no livro ainda existem - a Casa Havaneza, no Chiado, por exemplo. É possível seguir os diferentes percursos de Carlos ou do Ega pelas suas da Baixa lisboeta, ainda que algumas tenham mudado de nome. No final do livro, quando Carlos volta a Lisboa muitos anos depois, somos levados a ver as novidades - a Avenida da Liberdade, que substituiu o Passeio Público, e que é descrita como uma coisa nova, e feia pela sua novidade, exactamente como nos anos 70 se falava das casas de emigrante. O romance veicula sobre o país uma perspectiva muito derrotista, muito pessimista. Tirando a natureza (o Tejo, Sintra, Santa Olávia...), é tudo uma choldra ignóbil. Predomina uma visão de estrangeirado, de quem só valoriza as civilizações superiores - da França e Inglaterra, principalmente. Os políticos são mesquinhos, ignorantes ou corruptos (Gouvarinho, Sousa Neto...); os homens das Letras sao boémios e dissolutos, retrógrados ou distantes da realidade concreta (Alencar, Ega...: lembre-se o que se passou no Sarau do Teatro da Trindade); os jornalistas boémios e venais (Palma...); os homens do desporto não conseguem organizar uma corrida de cavalos, pois não há hipódromo à altura, nem cavalos, nem cavaleiros, as pessoas não vestem como o evento exigia, as senhoras traziam vestidos de missa. Para cúmulo de tudo isto, os protagonistas acabam vencidos da vida. Apesar de ser isto referido no fim do livro, pode-se ver que ainda há alguma esperanca implícita, nas passagens em que Carlos da Maia e João da Ega dizem que o apetite humano é a causa de todos os seus problemas e que portanto nunca mais terão apetites, mas logo a seguir dizem que lhes está a apetecer um &quot;prato de paio com ervilhas,&quot; ou quando dizem que a pressa não leva a nada e que a vida deve ser levada com calma mas começam a correr para apanhar o americano (eléctrico). Mais do que crítica de costumes, o romance mostra-nos um país - sobretudo Lisboa - que se dissolve, incapaz de se regenerar. Quando o autor escreve mais tarde A Cidade e as Serras, expõe uma atitude muito mais construtiva: o protagonista regenera-se pela descoberta das raízes rurais ancestrais não atingidas pela degradação da civilização, num movimento inverso ao que predomina n\'Os Maias.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7816966755593019147\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n Nathan points to periods of relative order and stability in international relations-the time immediately prior to the rise of Frederick the Great, for example, or the half century after the Napoleonic Wars-as times when states have been most vulnerable to spoilers and rogues. Only the power of the Cold War blocs fostered durable order. Now, notwithstanding novel elements of globalization, international relations appear as dependent as ever on the prudent management of force.\n\n\n\nStudents, scholars, and soldiers are frequently exposed to Clausewitz, Westphalia, Napoleon, World War I, and the like. But what makes these events and individuals so important? This book is Clausewitz\'s successor, insisting that soldiers and statesmen know and master the integrative potential of force. Nathan provides a narrative account of the people and events that have shaped international relations since the onset of the state system. He asserts that an understanding of the limits and utility of persuasion, as well as the corresponding limits and utility of force, will help assure national security in a world filled with more Senator Ben Sasse warns the nation about the existential threat to America\'s future.\nRaised by well-meaning but overprotective parents and coddled by well-meaning but misbegotten government programs, America\'s youth are ill-equipped to survive in our highly-competitive global economy. Many of the coming-of-age rituals that have defined the American experience since the Founding: learning the value of working with your hands, leaving home to start a family, becoming economically self-reliant—are being delayed or skipped altogether. The statistics are daunting: 30% of college students drop out after the first year, and only 4 in 10 graduate. One in three 18-to-34 year-olds live with their parents. From these disparate phenomena: Nebraska Senator Ben Sasse who as president of a Midwestern college observed the trials of this generation up close, sees an existential threat to the American way of life.In The Vanishing American Adult, Sasse diagnoses the causes of a generation that can\'t grow up and offers a path for raising children to become active and engaged citizens. He identifies core formative experiences that all young people should pursue: hard work to appreciate the benefits of labor, travel to understand deprivation and want, the power of reading, the importance of nurturing your body—and explains how parents can encourage them.Our democracy depends on responsible, contributing adults to function properly—without them America falls prey to populist demagogues. A call to arms, The Vanishing American Adult will ignite a much-needed debate about the link between the way we\'re raising our children and the future of our country.<\/span>\n  <a That nation did not exist when, in the mid-19th century, Europeans first began to explore the possibilities of creating a link between the Atlantic and Pacific oceans through the narrow but mountainous isthmus; Panama was then a remote and overlooked part of Colombia.\n\nAll that changed, writes David McCullough in his magisterial history of the Canal, in 1848, when prospectors struck gold in California. A wave of fortune seekers descended on Panama from Europe and the eastern United States, seeking quick passage on California-bound ships in the Pacific, and the Panama Railroad, built to serve that traffic, was soon the highest-priced stock listed on the New York Exchange. \n\nTo build a 51-mile-long ship canal to replace that railroad seemed an easy matter to some investors. But, as McCullough notes, the construction project came to involve the efforts of thousands of workers from many nations over four decades; eventually those workers, laboring in oppressive heat in a vast malarial swamp, removed enough soil and rock to build a pyramid a mile high. In the early years, they toiled under the direction of French entrepreneur Ferdinand de Lesseps, who went bankrupt while pursuing his dream of extending France\'s empire in the Americas. \n\nThe United States then entered the picture, with President Theodore Roosevelt orchestrating the purchase of the canal—but not before helping foment a revolution that removed Panama from Colombian rule and Drucker explains and interprets fascism and Nazism as fundamental revolutions. In some ways, this book anticipated by more than a decade the existentialism that came to dominate the European political mood in the postwar period. Drucker provides a special addition to the massive literature on existentialism and alienation since World War II. The End of Economic Man is a social and political effort to explain the subjective consequences of the social upheavals caused by warfare.\n\nDrucker concentrates on one specific historical event: the breakdown of the social and political structure of Europe which culminated in the rise of Nazi totalitarianism to mastery over Europe. He explains the tragedy of Europe as the loss of political faith, resulting from the political alienation of the European masses. The End of Economic Man is a book of great social import. It shows not only what might have helped the older generation avert the catastrophe of Nazism, but also how today\'s generation can prevent another such catastrophe. This work will be of special interest to political scientists, intellectual historians, and sociologists.\n\nThe book was singled out for praise on both sides of the Atlantic, and is considered by the But <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13977296219161885215\" style=\"display:none\">What are the grand dynamics that drive the accumulation and distribution of capital? Questions about the long-term evolution of inequality, the concentration of wealth, and the prospects for economic growth lie at the heart of political economy. But satisfactory answers have been hard to find for lack of adequate data and clear guiding theories. In Capital in the Twenty-First Century, Thomas Piketty analyzes a unique collection of data from twenty countries, ranging as far back as the eighteenth century, to uncover key economic and social patterns. His findings will transform debate and set the agenda for the next generation of thought about wealth and inequality.\n\nPiketty shows that modern economic growth and the diffusion of knowledge have allowed us to avoid inequalities on the apocalyptic scale predicted by Karl Marx. But we have not modified the deep structures of capital and inequality as much as we thought in the optimistic decades following World War II. The main driver of inequality—the tendency of returns on capital to exceed the rate of economic growth—today threatens to generate extreme inequalities that stir discontent and undermine democratic values. But economic trends are not acts of God. Political action has curbed dangerous inequalities in the past, Piketty says, and may do so again.\n\nA work of extraordinary ambition, originality, and rigor, Capital in the Twenty-First Century reorients our understanding of economic history and confronts us with sobering lessons for today.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13977296219161885215\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1395954963977305382">Sir Winston Leonard Spencer-Churchill, KG, OM, CH, TD, FRS, PC (Can) was a British politician who served as Prime Minister of the United Kingdom from 1940 to 1945, and again from 1951 to 1955. A noted statesman, orator and strategist, Churchill was also an officer in the British Army. A prolific author, he won the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1953 for his own historical writings, "for his mastery </span>
  <span id="freeText1395954963977305382" style="display:none">Sir Winston Leonard Spencer-Churchill, KG, OM, CH, TD, FRS, PC (Can) was a British politician who served as Prime Minister of the United Kingdom from 1940 to 1945, and again from 1951 to 1955. A noted statesman, orator and strategist, Churchill was also an officer in the British Army. A prolific author, he won the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1953 for his own historical writings, "for his mastery of historical and biographical description as well as for brilliant oratory in defending exalted human values."<br /><br />Out of respect for the well-known American author, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2834066.Winston_Churchill" rel="nofollow noopener">Winston Churchill</a>, Winston S. Churchill offered to use his middle initial in any works that he authored.</span>
